        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597"><meta property="og:title" content="CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c03597.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as promising candidates for energy storage applications due to the abundance and low cost of sodium. However, the larger radius of sodium ions limits their diffusion kinetics within electrode materials and contributes to electrode volume expansion. Here, we successfully synthesized porous titanium carbide (TiC) nanoflowers through chemical vapor deposition (CVD). The TiC nanoflowers exhibit exceptional electrochemical performance as SIB anodes, with their porous structure enhancing the conductivity, mechanical stability, and Na-ion diffusion. The TiC nanoflowers demonstrate a high reversible specific capacity of 73.5 mAh g–1 at 1 A g–1 after 2500 cycles, corresponding to an impressive capacity retention of 80.81%. Additionally, we developed a full sodium-ion cell utilizing TiC nanoflowers as the anode and Na3V2(PO4)3 as the cathode, which demonstrates a substantial reversible capacity and outstanding cycling stability. Our work presents a promising strategy for synthesizing nanostructured TiC materials as anode electrodes for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c03597.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187f738bb2075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f738bb2075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'b5e445d6-46a5-454b-b657-0499f9930e90'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187f738bb2075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187f738bb2075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "CVD-Synthesized Titanium Carbide Nanoflowers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/medium/nl4c03597_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as promising candidates for energy storage applications due to the abundance and low cost of sodium. However, the larger radius of sodium ions limits their diffusion kinetics within electrode materials and contributes to electrode volume expansion. Here, we successfully synthesized porous titanium carbide (TiC) nanoflowers through chemical vapor deposition (CVD). The TiC nanoflowers exhibit exceptional electrochemical performance as SIB anodes, with their porous structure enhancing the conductivity, mechanical stability, and Na-ion diffusion. The TiC nanoflowers demonstrate a high reversible specific capacity of 73.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 2500 cycles, corresponding to an impressive capacity retention of 80.81%. Additionally, we developed a full sodium-ion cell utilizing TiC nanoflowers as the anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the cathode, which demonstrates a substantial reversible capacity and outstanding cycling stability. Our work presents a promising strategy for synthesizing nanostructured TiC materials as anode electrodes for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D13324%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWei%2BDing%252C%2BJason%2BSternhagen%252C%2BLaxmi%2BRaj%2BJaishi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D24%26issueNum%3D42%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.4c03597%26title%3DCVD-Synthesized%2BTitanium%2BCarbide%2BNanoflowers%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D13332%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials"> Anode materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292088&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical vapor deposition"> Chemical vapor deposition </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291571&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Titanium"> Titanium </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Titanium+Carbide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Titanium%20Carbide">Titanium Carbide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Chemical+Vapor+Deposition&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Chemical%20Vapor%20Deposition%20%28CVD%29">Chemical Vapor Deposition (CVD)</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanoflower&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Nanoflower">Nanoflower</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Anode">Anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Sodium-Ion%20Battery%20%28SIB%29">Sodium-Ion Battery (SIB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are extensively used in portable/wearable electronics, electric vehicles, and grid energy storage due to their high energy density and long cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> However, concerns about the long-term sustainability and affordability of lithium resources present challenges. In contrast, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as promising alternatives to LIBs because of the abundance and lower cost of sodium, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> which is more widely distributed in the Earth’s crust, potentially making SIBs more sustainable and cost-effective. However, the larger radius of sodium ions limits their diffusion kinetics within electrode materials and contributes to electrode volume expansion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Therefore, a major challenge in the development of high-performance SIBs lies in creating electrodes that not only facilitate effective Na<sup>+</sup> transport within their structure but also maintain structural stability against the repeated volume changes occurring during the charging and discharging processes. The cycling life and durability of anode materials are critical for the longevity and efficiency of SIBs. Numerous anode materials such as carbon-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4−8)</a> alloy-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> metal oxide, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> metal sulfides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 12 to 15">(12−15)</a> and titanium-based materials <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> have been investigated as potential candidates for SIBs to date.</p><p>Titanium carbide (TiC) has gained significant interest among different transition metal carbides due to its unique properties, including its effectiveness as an anti-corrosion coating, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> high melting point (3067 °C), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> excellent thermal stability, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> relatively low density (4.93 g·cm<sup>–3</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and high electrical conductivity (3 × 10<sup>7</sup> S·cm<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> TiC  exhibits cubic crystal structure of the NaCl type. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> TiC synthesis can be achieved through carbothermal reduction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Mg-thermal reduction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> pyrolysis, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> self-propagating high-temperature synthesis, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> mechanical alloying, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> liquid phase sintering, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> and chemical vapor deposition (CVD). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> Typically, CVD synthesis of TiC involves the growth of amorphous TiC powders or films on various substrates such as Ta and graphite using H<sub>2</sub>, TiCl<sub>4</sub>, Ar, and carbon sources like CH<sub>4</sub> or C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub> at a high temperature in the range of 850–1050 °C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref36 ref38 ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref36 ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 23, 36 and 38 to 40">(23,36,38−40)</a> Besides amorphous TiC particles, nanostructured TiC whiskers <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and Ti<sub>2</sub>CCl<sub>2</sub> MXene carpets <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> were also reported. These studies highlight the significance of the CVD method in controlling the compositions and nanostructures of TiC.</p><p>TiC is naturally known as one of the choices for the anode materials of SIBs due to lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier and higher theoretical capacity (447 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> Recently, one-dimensional TiC nanorods have shown excellent reversible capacities of 173.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> In addition, the TiC anode materials exhibit a peculiar phenomenon whereby the specific capacity increases and then decreases slightly as the cycling continues. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> As sodium possesses  chemical properties similar to those of lithium, TiC could also serve as anode materials of SIBs. The TiC nanowires prepared by CVD showed good rate performance (135.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and excellent cyclic performance (retaining 90.14% capacity after 1000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>) as SIB anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Despite efforts to synthesize nanosized TiC powders, existing methods are often costly, complex, and difficult to control. Thus, developing an efficient method to produce a nanostructured TiC with high conductivity, porous structures, and excellent mechanical stability is crucial.</p><p>In this study, we successfully synthesized unique TiC nanoflowers with porous structures through a simple CVD approach using CH<sub>4</sub> as the carbon source, TiCl<sub>4</sub> and Ti foil as the titanium source, and Ar as the carrier gas. These CVD-synthesized TiC nanoflowers have a microscale diameter with nanoscale-thick petals. Their high surface-to-volume ratio, substantial electrical conductivity, strong mechanical strength, and excellent chemical stability position them as promising anode materials for SIBs. Our results demonstrate that the TiC nanoflower-based anodes exhibit impressive rate performance and outstanding cycling stability. Specifically, the TiC nanoflowers as anode material of SIBs displayed a capacity of 152.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> and a high-capacity retention of 80.81% after 2500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The reported strategy provides a potential way to synthesize nanostructured TiC as  high-performance anode materials of SIBs.</p><p>TiC nanoflowers were synthesized through a CVD method according to the process illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,b. CH<sub>4</sub> was used as the carbon source, while the titanium source included two parts, the gaseous TiCl<sub>4</sub> vapor and solid Ti foil. Ar was used as the inert carrier gas. The Ti foil also served as the substrate where the chemical reactions happened in the CVD process. In a typical CVD synthesis, the temperature of the CVD system gradually increased to the target temperature (usually 850–1050 °C) under Ar gas flow. Once the targeted temperature was reached, CH<sub>4</sub> and TiCl<sub>4</sub> were introduced to the CVD system for TiC growth for 2 h. Since TiCl<sub>4</sub> was liquid at room temperature,  Ar was bubbled through the TiCl<sub>4</sub> to bring the vapor to the CVD system.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/medium/nl4c03597_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. CVD synthesis of TiC nanoflowers and their morphological structures. (a) Schematic diagram illustrates the CVD synthesis of TiC nanoflowers. (b) Schematic diagram shows the process of TiC nanoflower formation, illustrating the transformation of nanosheets into nanoflowers. (c) XRD patterns of Ti foil and TiC nanoflowers. (d, e) SEM images showing the morphology of TiC nanoflowers synthesized at 950 °C. (f) Raman spectrum of TiC nanoflowers. (g) High-resolution TEM image of the TiC nanoflowers. (h) The corresponding SAED pattern.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns for both the Ti foil and TiC nanoflowers. For the Ti foil, the distinctive peaks of (100), (002), (101), (102), (110), and (103) are observable. However, after a CVD synthesis duration of 120 min, the original Ti foil had fully reacted, with no remaining Ti-associated peaks. As a result, the distinct peaks of (111), (200), (220), and (311) have emerged, confirming the successful formation of TiC, which aligns perfectly with the standard cubic TiC structure (JCPDF 71-0289). The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images, displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d,e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, show the morphologies of TiC samples synthesized at different growth temperatures (850–1050 °C). The images reveal  nanoflower morphology across all samples. At 850 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a,d</a>), TiC nanomaterials exhibit a sparse distribution of fine, fibrous nanoflowers due to limited atomic mobility. Increasing the temperature to 950 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b,d</a>) results in denser and thicker nanoflowers with more defined structures due to enhanced atomic mobility. At 1050 °C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c,e</a>), the nanoflowers are densely packed and thickly layered, reflecting significant growth from higher atomic mobility and reaction rates. These structural changes suggest the thermally activated nature of TiC nanoflower growth during the CVD process with higher temperatures, leading to more pronounced nanoflower structures. To examine the growth duration’s impact on TiC morphology, growth times varied from 30 to 120 min at 950 °C. SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) show nanosheets evolving into interconnected, porous nanoflowers, demonstrating the CVD process’s effectiveness under these conditions.</p><p>Conventionally, the CVD synthesis of TiC involves the formation of amorphous TiC substances, ranging from powders to films, upon substrates such as tantalum, graphite, and steel. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref40 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref40 ref47" aria-label="reference 36, 40 and 47">(36,40,47)</a> In these CVD systems, the TiCl<sub>4</sub> precursor is the only source of titanium. However, in our CVD system, the titanium source includes two parts, TiCl<sub>4</sub> in the gas phase and Ti foil in the solid phase. It is reported that TiC can also be in situ formed via gas–solid chemical reaction between CH<sub>4</sub> and Ti at elevated temperature higher than 700 °C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Thus, we believe the following two chemical reactions both occur in our CVD system.</p><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-5"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-6"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-7" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-8">TiCl</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-9" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-10">4</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-11" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-12"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-13"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-14" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-15">CH</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-16" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-17">4</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-18"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-19" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">→</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-20"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-21"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-22">TiC</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-23" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-24"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-25">4</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-26">HCl</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>TiCl</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub></mrow><mo>+</mo><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>CH</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mrow><mi>TiC</mi></mrow><mo>+</mo><mrow><mn>4</mn><mi>HCl</mi></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-27"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-28"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-29"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-30"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-31">Ti</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-32" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-33"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-34"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-35" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-36">CH</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-37" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-38">4</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-39"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-40" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">→</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-41"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-42">TiC</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-43" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-44"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-45"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-46" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-47">2</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-48">H</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-49" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-50">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>Ti</mi><mo>+</mo><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>CH</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>TiC</mi><mo>+</mo><mrow><msub><mrow><mn>2</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div><p></p><p>At the initial CVD stage, CH<sub>4</sub> reacts with Ti foil, forming a TiC layer due to its lower activation energy than TiCl<sub>4</sub>. As the TiC layer thickens, the reaction becomes diffusion-controlled, with CH<sub>4</sub> diffusion through the TiC limiting the process. Eventually, the gas-phase reaction between TiCl<sub>4</sub> and CH<sub>4</sub> dominates. The growing TiC layer induces in-plane mechanical stress, relieved by wrinkling or buckling, forming the initial nanoflower structures. These deformations create active sites for further TiC nucleation and growth, facilitating the gas-phase deposition of the TiC nanoflowers.</p><p>In addition, Raman spectroscopy was done to study the formation of TiC materials. According to <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f, three peaks have been observed at 631 cm<sup>–1</sup>, 422 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and 268 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which are consistent with Raman scattering in TiC materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 49 to 51">(49−51)</a> The images of transmission electron microscopy (TEM), high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), and selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g, and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h. The TiC nanoflowers were created via the stacking of TiC nanoflakes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g, there were two lattice fringes of <i>d</i> = 0.249  and 0.215 nm in the TiC samples, which correspond to the (111) plane and the (200) plane of the TiC phase, respectively. The SAED patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h) displayed four diffraction rings from inner to outer, corresponding to the planes of (111), (200), (220), and (311) of cubic TiC. The result from TEM matches well with the XRD data.</p><p>The homogeneity of elements Ti, C, and Cl within the TiC samples is illustrated through elemental mapping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). The composition of the TiC powders was investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis. The survey spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) show the presence of titanium, carbon, and chloride. Five peaks can be gained in high-resolution Ti 2p spectra in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b. Peaks at 454.38, 456.13, and 460. 58 eV are associated with Ti–C bonds, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> while the peak at 459.28 eV is ascribed to Ti<sup>0</sup>(2p<sup>1/2</sup>). In addition, the binding energy peak at 455.14 eV is associated with a Ti–O bond, which is derived from the oxidation of titanium slightly. Binding energy peaks at 281.25, 284.68, 286.38, and 288.68 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c) are ascribed to C–Ti, C–C, C–O, and C–Cl bonds, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d illustrates the Cl 1s spectra for  TiC, showing peaks at 198.69 and 200.33 eV, which correspond to the bonds of Cl–Ti and Cl–C, respectively, suggesting the incorporation of chloride within the TiC structure.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/medium/nl4c03597_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Characterization of the TiC nanoflowers. (a) Element mapping of the TiC nanoflowers using SEM-EDS. (b–d) Ti 2p, C 1s, and Cl 2p XPS spectra of TiC nanoflowers.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of the TiC nanoflower anode was investigated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves at scanning rates from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within 0.01–3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> were used to evaluate the pseudocapacitive contribution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). The pseudocapacitive contribution was calculated based on  eq 1 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d reveals that the slopes (<i><b>b1</b></i> and <i><b>b2</b></i>) obtained by fitting peak 1 (reduction) and peak 2 (oxidation) on the CV curve of the TiC nanoflower electrode are 0.57 and 0.90, respectively, indicating a predominantly diffusion-controlled Na<sup>+</sup> storage process. The capacitive contribution increased from 74.5% to 85.6% as the scan rates increased from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b and c), highlighting fast kinetics at high charge–discharge rates. The linear relationship between <i><b>i</b></i><sub><i><b>p</b></i></sub> and <i><b>v</b></i><sup><i><b>1/2</b></i></sup> from peak 2 in the CV curves was confirmed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e), with a slope of 0.101. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis was performed on a TiC nanoflower electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f). The equivalent circuit model and its parameters used for fitting the EIS data are detailed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Figure 9 and Supporting Information, Table 1</a>. The corresponding equivalent circuit diagrams are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Figure 9b</a>. It is evident that the values of ohmic resistance (<i><b>R</b></i><sub><i><b>e</b></i></sub>), contact resistance (<i><b>R</b></i><sub><i><b>f</b></i></sub>), and charge-transfer reaction resistance (<i><b>R</b></i><sub><i><b>ct</b></i></sub>) for the TiC nanoflower anode are higher before cycling than after 100 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting an activation process that enhances the interaction between the electrode and electrolyte. Additionally, the diffusion coefficient of Na ions within the electrode is approximately 1.26 × 10<sup>–17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/medium/nl4c03597_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of the TiC nanoflower anode. (a) CV curves of the TiC nanoflower anode at different scan rates from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Fitting areas of the CV curves. (c) Corresponding capacitive contribution ratios at various scanning rates (0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>). (d) Relation between the peak current and scan rate. (e) Linear fitting of the TiC nanoflower anode based on the peak in the CV curves. (f) EIS spectra. (g) Rate performance. (h) Charge–discharge curves of the TiC nanoflower anode at different current densities. (i) Cycling performance of the TiC nanoflower anode at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 2500 charge/discharge cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The rate capabilities of the TiC nanoflower electrode were tested by galvanostatic charge–discharge at 0.05 to 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g). At 0.05 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode showed reversible specific capacities of 162.7 and 60.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. At 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it delivered a specific capacity of 43.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating superior rate performance. Despite a decrease in capacity with increasing current density, the charge–discharge curves remained stable, indicating good rate performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h). After 2500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a high capacity of 73.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was retained, with 80.81% capacity retention (average capacity decay of only 0.0077% per cycle) and stable Coulombic efficiency (reached 100% by the third cycle and remained stable thereafter), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i. Additionally, the cycle stability and morphology of the TiC anode were tested after 100 cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S14 and S15</a>. The SEM observations reveal that the TiC anode maintains a tight and uniform structure.</p><p>To understand the electrochemical redox mechanisms of TiC nanoflowers, SEM and EDS mapping images of the TiC nanoflower electrode were captured before (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a) and after (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b) long-term cycling tests. Initially, the Ti and C elements were uniformly distributed. After 100 cycles, Ti, C, and Na remained evenly distributed, with gaps and voids disappearing, indicating activation and self-healing during cycling. CV curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c) for the first two cycles showed significant differences, with broad reduction peaks below 1.0 V in the first cycle due to irreversible reactions and SEI layer formation. Since Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> have similar chemical properties, we also performed a systematic Li<sup>+</sup> storage behavior study with the TiC nanoflower (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information Figures S5–S9</a>). CV curves revealed redox peaks at ∼0.009 and ∼0.176 V for LIBs and at 0.014 and 0.136 V for SIBs. The potential difference of SIBs (Δ<sub>SIBs</sub> = 0.122 V) is lower than LIBs (Δ<sub>LIBs</sub> = 0.167 V), indicating TiC has a lower polarization and a higher reversibility during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, compared to Li<sup>+</sup> intercalation. Compared with other titanium carbide-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref53" aria-label="reference 46 and 53">(46,53)</a> the TiC nanoflowers stand out for their exceptional rate capability and superior cycling capacity and stability in SIBs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/suppl_file/nl4c03597_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Table S4</a>).</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/medium/nl4c03597_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> storage on the TiC nanoflower anode. (a) SEM image and the EDS mapping result of TiC nanoflower electrode before the Na<sup>+</sup> storage test. (b) SEM image and the EDS mapping result of the same TiC nanoflower electrode after 100 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs. (c) CV curve of SIBs for first and second cycles. (d) Rate capacity comparison of the TiC nanoflower electrode with those of literature-reported TiC electrodes for SIBs. (e) Schematic of insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> on the TiC nanoflower anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597/asset/images/large/nl4c03597_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03597&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Numerous studies have utilized TiC as a conductive matrix in nanocomposite anodes to promote the electrochemical performance of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 54 to 56">(54−56)</a> Theoretical investigation has predicted that monolayer TiC has moderate adsorption strengths for Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>, contributing to ultrahigh energy densities and significant ionic conductivity with ultralow diffusion barriers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Moreover, the volume expansion observed in fully intercalated TiC is remarkably low, indicating a superior cycling stability. Shirvani et al. investigated the effects of alloying lithium with two-dimensional TiC and revealed negative cohesive energy values for Li-TiC alloy compositions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Yang et al. demonstrated the outstanding electrochemical performance of TiC nanomaterials as anode materials for LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Ren et al. proved the long cycle life of TiC anodes in LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Based on these findings, we propose the following Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism for the TiC nanoflower anode: During discharge, sodium ions migrate from the cathode, traverse the electrolyte, and intercalate into the TiC nanoflower anode, driven by an electrochemical potential difference. During charge, sodium ions deintercalate and return to the cathode. Additionally, TiC nanoflowers undergo a de/alloying conversion process, enhancing sodium-ion storage and release dynamics. The overall electrochemical reaction can be simplified as follows:</p><p>Discharge (Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into TiC nanoflowers):</p><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-51"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-52"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-53"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-54"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-55"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-56">T</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-57">i</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-58">C</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-59" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-60">x</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-61"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-62" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-63">N</span><span class="MJXp-mi" class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-67" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-68">x</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-69"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-70" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-71">e</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-72" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-73">−</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-74"><span class="MJXp-mo"
